</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Fusion power</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sun_in_X-Ray.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sun_in_X-Ray.png/300px-Sun_in_X-Ray.png" width="300" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sun_in_X-Ray.png/450px-Sun_in_X-Ray.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sun_in_X-Ray.png/600px-Sun_in_X-Ray.png 2x" data-file-width="690" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sun_in_X-Ray.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> is a natural fusion reactor, using <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> to transform lighter elements into heavier elements plus energy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Fusion power</b> is <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> generated by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. Fusion reactions fuse two lighter <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> to form a heavier nucleus. It is a major area of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Plasma physics">plasma physics</a> research that attempts to harness such reactions as a source of large scale <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a>. Fusion reactions are how <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> transmute matter into energy.</p>
<p>In most large scale commercial programs, <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">neutron scattering</a> in a controlled reaction is used to operate a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> that drives <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generators</a>. Many fusion concepts are under investigation. The current leading designs are the <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement</a> by <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>. As of February 2017, these technologies were not currently viable, as they cannot produce more energy than is required to initiate and sustain a fusion reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alternative approaches rely on other means of energy transfer, mostly that capture energy without <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion">relying on neutron capture</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/300px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" width="300" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/450px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/600px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="671" data-file-height="441" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Binding energy for different atoms. Iron-56 has the highest, making it the most stable. Atoms to the left are likely to fuse; atoms to the right are likely to split.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fusion reactions occur when two (or more) atomic nuclei come close enough for long enough that the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong nuclear force</a> pulling them together exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a> pushing them apart, fusing them into heavier nuclei. For nuclei lighter than <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">iron-56</a>, the reaction is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a>, releasing energy. For nuclei heavier than iron-56, the reaction is <a href="/wiki/Endothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a>, requiring an external source of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Hence, nuclei smaller than iron-56 are more likely to fuse while those heavier than iron-56 are more likely to break apart.</p>
<p>The strong force acts only over short distances. The repulsive electrostatic force acts over longer distances, so <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> is needed to overcome this "<a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">Coulomb barrier</a>" before the reaction can take place. Ways of doing this include speeding up atoms in a <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerator</a>, or heating them to high temperatures.</p>
<p>Once an atom is heated above its <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> energy, its <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> are stripped away (it is ionized), leaving just the bare nucleus (the <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>). The result is a hot cloud of ions and the electrons formerly attached to them. This cloud is known as a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>. Because the charges are separated, plasmas are electrically conductive and magnetically controllable. Many fusion devices take advantage of this to control the particles as they are heated.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross_Section">Cross Section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cross Section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/300px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/450px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/600px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="405" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The fusion reaction rate increases rapidly with temperature until it maximizes and then gradually drops off. The deuterium-tritium fusion rate peaks at a lower temperature (about 70&#160;keV, or 800 million kelvin) and at a higher value than other reactions commonly considered for fusion energy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A reaction's <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a>, denoted σ, is the measure of the probability that a fusion reaction will happen. This depends on the relative velocity of the two nuclei. Higher relative velocities increase the probability. Cross sections for many fusion reactions were measured (mainly in the 1970s) using <a href="/wiki/Particle_beam" title="Particle beam">particle beams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-osti.gov_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osti.gov-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a plasma, particle velocity can be characterized using a probability distribution. If the plasma is thermalized, the distribution looks like a <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">bell curve</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">maxwellian distribution</a>. In this case, it is useful to take the average cross section over the velocity distribution. This is entered into the volumetric fusion rate:<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-4">[4]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawson_Criterion">Lawson Criterion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lawson Criterion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Lawson Criterion shows how energy varies with temperature, density, speed of collision and fuel. This equation was central to John Lawson's analysis of fusion working with a hot plasma. Lawson assumed an <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">energy balance</a>, shown below.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<dl>

    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

          </mrow>
        </msub>

        <msub>

        </msub>
        <mrow>
          <mo>(</mo>

</dl>

</ul>
<p>Plasma clouds lose energy through <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conduction" title="Thermal conduction">conduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-4">[4]</a></sup> Conduction occurs when <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutral_particle" title="Neutral particle">neutrals</a> impact a surface and transfer a portion of their kinetic energy to the atoms of the surface. Radiation is energy that leaves the cloud as light in the visible, <a href="/wiki/UV" class="mw-redirect" title="UV">UV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">IR</a>, or <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> spectra. Radiation increases with temperature. Fusion power technologies must overcome these losses.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triple_product:_density.2C_temperature.2C_time">Triple product: density, temperature, time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Triple product: density, temperature, time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Lawson criterion argues that a machine holding a <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">thermalized</a> and quasi-<a href="/wiki/Neutral_particle" title="Neutral particle">neutral</a> plasma has to meet basic criteria to overcome <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> losses, <a href="/wiki/Conduction_(heat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conduction (heat)">conduction</a> losses and reach efficiency of 30 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This became known as the "triple product": the plasma density, temperature and confinement time.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Attempts to increase the triple product led to targeting larger plants. Larger plants move structural materials further away from the centre of the plasma, which reduces conduction and radiation losses since more of the radiation is internally reflected. This emphasis on <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mi>T</mi>
        <mi>&#x03C4;<!-- τ --></mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (nT\tau )}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/776ae3823d016008ccaaadab92f5ccb4a0566214" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.094ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (nT\tau )}" /></span> as a metric of success has impacted other considerations such as cost, size, complexity and efficiency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2014)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Fusion_power#there.27s_zero_.22efficiency.22_unless_fusion_is_reached.2C_and_experimental_scientists_are_unlikely_to_put_an_abstract_number_above_actual_results.2C_if_they_don.27t_believe_it_is_the_decisive_quantity_to_achieve_fusion" title="Talk:Fusion power">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> This has led to larger, more complicated and more expensive machines such as <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">NIF</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_behavior">Plasma behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Plasma behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Plasma is an ionized gas that conducts electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In bulk, it is modeled using <a href="/wiki/Hydrodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrodynamics">hydrodynamics</a>, which is a combination of the <a href="/wiki/Navier-Stokes" class="mw-redirect" title="Navier-Stokes">Navier-Stokes</a> equations governing fluids and <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell's equations</a> governing how magnetic and electric fields behave.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Fusion exploits several plasma properties, including:</p>
<p>Self-organizing plasma conducts electric and magnetic fields. Its motions can generate fields that can in turn contain it.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">Diamagnetic plasma</a> can generate its own internal magnetic field. This can reject an externally applied magnetic field, making it diamagnetic.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">Magnetic mirrors</a> can reflect plasma when it moves from a low to high density field.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_capture">Energy capture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Energy capture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Multiple approaches have been proposed for energy capture. The simplest is to heat a fluid. The neutrons generated by fusion can re-generate a spent fission fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Direct_energy_conversion" title="Direct energy conversion">Direct energy conversion</a> was developed (at <a href="/wiki/LLNL" class="mw-redirect" title="LLNL">LLNL</a> in the 1980s) as a method to maintain a voltage using the fusion reaction products. This has demonstrated energy capture efficiency of 48 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_confinement">Magnetic confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Magnetic confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">Tokamak</a>: the most well-developed and well-funded approach to fusion energy. This method races hot plasma around in a magnetically confined, donut-shaped ring, with an internal current. When completed, <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> will be the world's largest tokamak. As of April 2012 an estimated 215 experimental tokamaks were either planned, decommissioned or currently operating (35) worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Spherical_tokamak" title="Spherical tokamak">Spherical tokamak</a>:</i> A variation on the tokamak with a spherical shape.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Stellarator" title="Stellarator">Stellarator</a>:</i> Twisted rings of hot plasma. The stellarator attempts to create a natural twist plasma path, using external magnets, while tokamaks create those magnetic fields using an internal current. Stellarators were developed by <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer" title="Lyman Spitzer">Lyman Spitzer</a> in 1950 and have four designs: Torsatron, Heliotron, Heliac and Helias. One example is <a href="/wiki/Wendelstein_7-X" title="Wendelstein 7-X">Wendelstein 7-X</a>, a German fusion device that produced its first plasma on December 10, 2015. It is the world's largest stellarator,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> designed to investigate the suitability of this type of device for a power station.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Levitated_dipole" title="Levitated dipole">Levitated Dipole Experiment (LDX)</a>:</i> These use a solid superconducting torus. This is magnetically levitated inside the reactor chamber. The superconductor forms an axisymmetric magnetic field that contains the plasma. The LDX was developed by MIT and Columbia University after 2000 by Jay Kesner and Michael E. Mauel.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">Magnetic mirror</a>:</i> Developed by <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Post" title="Richard F. Post">Richard F. Post</a> and teams at <a href="/wiki/LLNL" class="mw-redirect" title="LLNL">LLNL</a> in the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Mirror_Systems_1969_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirror_Systems_1969-17">[17]</a></sup> Magnetic mirrors reflected hot plasma back and forth in a line. Variations included the magnetic bottle and the <a href="/wiki/Biconic_cusp" title="Biconic cusp">biconic cusp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> A series of well-funded, large, mirror machines were built by the US government in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Field-reversed_configuration" title="Field-reversed configuration">Field-reversed configuration</a>:</i> This device traps plasma in a self-organized quasi-stable structure; where the particle motion makes an internal magnetic field which then traps itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Freidberg2007_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freidberg2007-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Reversed_field_pinch" title="Reversed field pinch">Reversed field pinch</a>:</i> Here the plasma moves inside a ring. It has an internal magnetic field. Moving out from the center of this ring, the magnetic field reverses direction.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_confinement">Inertial confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Inertial confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Direct drive</a>:</i> In this technique, lasers directly blast a pellet of fuel. The goal is to ignite a fusion chain reaction. Ignition was first suggested by John Nuckolls, in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuckolls.2C_John_1972_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuckolls.2C_John_1972-21">[21]</a></sup> Notable direct drive experiments have been conducted at the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_for_Laser_Energetics" title="Laboratory for Laser Energetics">Laboratory for Laser Energetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser_M%C3%A9gajoule" title="Laser Mégajoule">Laser Mégajoule</a> and the <a href="/wiki/GEKKO_XII" title="GEKKO XII">GEKKO XII</a> facilities. Good implosions require fuel pellets with close to a perfect shape in order to generate a symmetrical inward <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> that produces the high-density plasma.</p>
<p><i>Fast ignition:</i> This method uses two laser blasts. The first blast compresses the fusion fuel, while the second high energy pulse ignites it. Experiments have been conducted at the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_for_Laser_Energetics" title="Laboratory for Laser Energetics">Laboratory for Laser Energetics</a> using the Omega and Omega EP systems and at the GEKKO XII laser at the Institute for Laser Engineering in Osaka Japan.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Indirect drive</a>:</i> In this technique, lasers blasts a structure around the pellet of fuel. This structure is known as a <a href="/wiki/Hohlraum" title="Hohlraum">Hohlraum</a>. As it disintegrates the pellet is bathed in a more uniform <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">x-ray</a> light, creating better compression. The largest system using this method is the <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">National Ignition Facility</a>.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Magneto-inertial_fusion" title="Magneto-inertial fusion">Magneto-inertial fusion</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Magnetized_Liner_Inertial_Fusion" title="Magnetized Liner Inertial Fusion">Magnetized Liner Inertial Fusion</a>:</i> This combines a laser pulse with a magnetic pinch. The pinch community refers to it as magnetized liner Inertial fusion while the ICF community refers to it as magneto-inertial fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-IFSA2007_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFSA2007-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Heavy Ion Beams</i> There are also proposals to do inertial confinement fusion with ion beams instead of laser beams.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The main difference is the mass of the beam has momentum, whereas lasers do not.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_or_electric_pinches">Magnetic or electric pinches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Magnetic or electric pinches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pinch_(plasma_physics)" title="Pinch (plasma physics)">Pinch (plasma physics)</a></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Z-Pinch" class="mw-redirect" title="Z-Pinch">Z-Pinch</a>:</i> This method sends a strong current (in the z-direction) through the plasma. The current generates a magnetic field that squeezes the plasma to fusion conditions. Pinches were the first method for man-made controlled fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-Seife.2C_Charles_2008_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seife.2C_Charles_2008-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Phillips.2C_James_2013_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips.2C_James_2013-25">[25]</a></sup> Some examples include the <a href="/wiki/Dense_plasma_focus" title="Dense plasma focus">Dense plasma focus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Z_Pulsed_Power_Facility" title="Z Pulsed Power Facility">Z machine</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a>.</p>
<p><i>Theta-Pinch:</i> This method sends a current inside a plasma, in the theta direction.</p>
<p><i>Screw Pinch:</i> This method combines a theta and z-pinch for improved stabilization.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_electrostatic_confinement">Inertial electrostatic confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Inertial electrostatic confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">Fusor</a>:</i> This method uses an electric field to heat ions to fusion conditions. The machine typically uses two spherical cages, a cathode inside the anode, inside a vacuum. These machines are not considered a viable approach to net power because of their high <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> losses. They are simple enough to build that amateurs have fused atoms using them.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">Polywell</a>:</i> This design attempts to combine magnetic confinement with electrostatic fields, to avoid the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conduction</a> losses generated by the cage.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Magnetized_target_fusion" title="Magnetized target fusion">Magnetized target fusion</a>:</i> This method confines hot plasma using a magnetic field and squeezes it using inertia. Examples include <a href="/wiki/LANL" class="mw-redirect" title="LANL">LANL</a> FRX-L machine,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Fusion" title="General Fusion">General Fusion</a> and the plasma liner experiment.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Project_PACER" title="Project PACER">Uncontrolled</a>:</i> Fusion has been initiated by man, using uncontrolled fission explosions to ignite so-called Hydrogen Bombs. Early proposals for fusion power included using bombs to initiate reactions.</p>
<p><i>Beam fusion:</i> A beam of high energy particles can be fired at another beam or target and fusion will occur. This was used in the 1970s and 1980s to study the cross sections of high energy fusion reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-osti.gov_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osti.gov-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Bubble_fusion" title="Bubble fusion">Bubble fusion</a>:</i> This was a fusion reaction that was supposed to occur inside extraordinarily large collapsing gas bubbles, created during acoustic liquid cavitation.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> This approach was discredited.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Cold_fusion" title="Cold fusion">Cold fusion</a>:</i> This is a hypothetical type of nuclear reaction that would occur at, or near, room temperature. Cold fusion is discredited and gained a reputation as <a href="/wiki/Pathological_science" title="Pathological science">pathological science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Muon-catalyzed_fusion" title="Muon-catalyzed fusion">Muon-catalyzed fusion</a>:</i> Muons allow atoms to get much closer and thus reduce the kinetic energy required to initiate fusion. Muons require more energy to produce than can be obtained from muon-catalysed fusion, making this approach impractical for power generation.<sup id="cite_ref-Huizenga1993_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huizenga1993-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">Gravitational-confinement fusion (GCF) Direct Photo-Electric Conversion</a>:</i> Also known as <a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">Space-Based Solar Power</a> argues that a majority of available fusion fuels exists within the sphere of the Sun where it is gravitationally confined, and that a tractable way to accomplish large-scale fusion power is to build very large space-borne platforms that capture energy via photons rather than via a <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">carnot cycle</a>. The theoretical limit of producing power by such means is a <a href="/wiki/Kardashev_scale" title="Kardashev scale">type-2 civilization</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Dyson_sphere" title="Dyson sphere">Dyson Sphere.</a></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_tools">Common tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Common tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heating">Heating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Heating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gas must be first heated to form a plasma. This then needs to be hot enough to start fusion reactions. A number of heating schemes have been explored:<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Dielectric_heating" title="Dielectric heating">Radiofrequency Heating</a></i> A radio wave is applied to the plasma, causing it to oscillate. This is basically the same concept as a <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a>. This is also known as <a href="/wiki/Electron_cyclotron_resonance_heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron cyclotron resonance heating">electron cyclotron resonance heating</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_heating" title="Dielectric heating">Dielectric heating</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">Electrostatic Heating</a></i> An electric field can do <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">work</a> on charged ions or electrons, heating them.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Neutral_Beam_Injection" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutral Beam Injection">Neutral Beam Injection</a></i> An external source of hydrogen is ionized and accelerated by an electric field to form a charged beam which is shone through a source of neutral hydrogen gas towards the plasma which itself is ionized and contained in the reactor by a magnetic field. Some of the intermediate hydrogen gas is accelerated towards the plasma by collisions with the charged beam while remaining neutral: this neutral beam is thus unaffected by the magnetic field and so shines through it into the plasma. Once inside the plasma the neutral beam transmits energy to the plasma by collisions as a result of which it becomes ionized and thus contained by the magnetic field thereby both heating and refuelling the reactor in one operation. The remainder of the charged beam is diverted by magnetic fields onto cooled beam dumps.</p>
<p><i>Magnetic Oscillations</i><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Thomson_Scattering" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomson Scattering">Thomson Scattering</a></i> Light scatters from plasma. This light can be detected and used to reconstruct the plasmas' behavior. This technique can be used to find its density and temperature. It is common in <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Inertial confinement fusion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">Tokamaks</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusors</a>. In ICF systems, this can be done by firing a second beam into a gold foil adjacent to the target. This makes x-rays that scatter or traverse the plasma. In Tokamaks, this can be done using mirrors and detectors to reflect light across a plane (two dimensions) or in a line (one dimension).</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Langmuir_probe" title="Langmuir probe">Langmuir probe</a></i> This is a metal object placed in a plasma. A potential is applied to it, giving it a positive or negative <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> against the surrounding plasma. The metal collects charged particles, drawing a current. As the voltage changes, the current changes. This makes a <a href="/wiki/Current%E2%80%93voltage_characteristic" title="Current–voltage characteristic">IV Curve</a>. The IV-curve can be used to determine the local plasma density, potential and temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Geiger_counter" title="Geiger counter">Geiger counter</a></i> Deuterium or tritium fusion produces neutrons. Geiger counters record the rate of neutron production, so they are an essential tool for demonstrating success.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Flux_loop" title="Flux loop">Flux loop</a></i> A loop of wire is inserted into the magnetic field. As the field passes through the loop, a current is made. The current is measured and used to find the total magnetic flux through that loop. This has been used on the <a href="/wiki/National_Compact_Stellarator_Experiment" title="National Compact Stellarator Experiment">National Compact Stellarator Experiment</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">polywell</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Levitated_dipole" title="Levitated dipole">LDX</a> machines.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/X-ray_detector" title="X-ray detector">X-ray detector</a></i> All plasma loses energy by emitting light. This covers the whole spectrum: visible, IR, UV, and X-rays. This occurs anytime a particle changes speed, <a href="/wiki/Larmor_formula" title="Larmor formula">for any reason.</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> If the reason is deflection by a magnetic field, the radiation is <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">Cyclotron</a> radiation at low speeds and <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron" title="Synchrotron">Synchrotron</a> radiation at high speeds. If the reason is deflection by another particle, plasma radiates X-rays, known as <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">Bremsstrahlung</a> radiation. X-rays are termed in both hard and soft, based on their energy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_production">Power production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Power production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">Steam turbines</a></i> It has been proposed <sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> that steam turbines be used to convert the heat from the fusion chamber into electricity. The heat is transferred into a <a href="/wiki/Working_fluid" title="Working fluid">working fluid</a> that turns into steam, driving electric generators.</p>
<p><i>Neutron blankets</i> Deuterium and tritium fusion generates <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>. This varies by technique (NIF has a record of 3E14 neutrons per second<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> while a typical <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a> produces 1E5–1E9 neutrons per second). It has been proposed to use these neutrons as a way to regenerate spent fission fuel <sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> or as a way to breed tritium using a breeder blanket consisting of liquid <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> or, as in more recent reactor designs, a helium cooled pebble bed consisting of lithium bearing ceramic pebbles fabricated from materials such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_titanate" title="Lithium titanate">Lithium titanate</a>, lithium orthosilicate or mixtures of these phases.<sup id="cite_ref-l2s_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-l2s-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Direct_energy_conversion" title="Direct energy conversion">Direct conversion</a></i> This is a method where the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of a particle is converted into <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> It was first suggested by <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Post" title="Richard F. Post">Richard F. Post</a> in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">magnetic mirrors</a>, in the late sixties. It has also been suggested for <a href="/wiki/Field-Reversed_Configuration" class="mw-redirect" title="Field-Reversed Configuration">Field-Reversed Configurations</a>. The process takes the plasma, expands it, and converts a large fraction of the random energy of the fusion products into directed motion. The particles are then collected on electrodes at various large electrical potentials. This method has demonstrated an experimental efficiency of 48 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Confinement">Confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg/250px-IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg.png" width="250" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg/375px-IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg/500px-IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="850" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IFE_and_MFE_parameter_space.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Parameter space occupied by <a href="/wiki/Inertial_fusion_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Inertial fusion energy">inertial fusion energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_fusion_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetic fusion energy">magnetic fusion energy</a> devices as of the mid 1990s. The regime allowing thermonuclear ignition with high gain lies near the upper right corner of the plot.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Confinement refers to all the conditions necessary to keep a plasma dense and hot long enough to undergo fusion. Here are some general principles.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mechanical_equilibrium" title="Mechanical equilibrium">Equilibrium</a>: The forces acting on the plasma must be balanced for containment. One exception is <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement</a>, where the relevant physics must occur faster than the disassembly time.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plasma_stability" title="Plasma stability">Stability</a>: The plasma must be so constructed so that disturbances will not lead to the plasma disassembling.</li>
<li>Transport or <a href="/wiki/Conduction_(heat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conduction (heat)">conduction</a>: The loss of material must be sufficiently slow.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-4">[4]</a></sup> The plasma carries off energy with it, so rapid loss of material will disrupt any machines power balance. Material can be lost by transport into different regions or <a href="/wiki/Conduction_(heat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conduction (heat)">conduction</a> through a solid or liquid.</li>
</ul>
<p>To produce self-sustaining fusion, the energy released by the reaction (or at least a fraction of it) must be used to heat new reactant nuclei and keep them hot long enough that they also undergo fusion reactions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconfined">Unconfined</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Unconfined">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first human-made, large-scale fusion reaction was the test of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a>, in 1952. As part of the <a href="/wiki/PACER_(fusion)" class="mw-redirect" title="PACER (fusion)">PACER</a> project, it was once proposed to use hydrogen bombs as a source of power by detonating them in underground caverns and then generating electricity from the heat produced, but such a power station is unlikely ever to be constructed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_confinement_2">Magnetic confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Magnetic confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the temperatures required for fusion, the fuel is heated to a plasma state. In this state it has a very good <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>. This opens the possibility of confining the plasma with <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>. This is the case of magnetized plasma, where the magnetic fields and plasma intermix. This is generally known as <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_confinement_fusion" title="Magnetic confinement fusion">magnetic confinement</a>. The field lines put a <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">Lorentz force</a> on the plasma. The force works perpendicular to the magnetic fields, so one problem in magnetic confinement is preventing the plasma from leaking out the ends of the field lines. A general measure of magnetic trapping in fusion is the beta ratio:</p>
<p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \beta ={\frac {p}{p_{mag}}}={\frac {nk_{B}T}{(B^{2}/2\mu _{0})}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a7eb0ebcc72f9d3aa72c52ced56e1b1a7db6eb36" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:23.254ex; height:6.176ex;" alt="\beta ={\frac {p}{p_{mag}}}={\frac {nk_{B}T}{(B^{2}/2\mu _{0})}}" /></span> <sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is the ratio of the externally applied field to the internal pressure of the plasma. A value of 1 is ideal trapping. Some examples of beta values include:</p>
<ol>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Small_Tight_Aspect_Ratio_Tokamak" title="Small Tight Aspect Ratio Tokamak">START</a> machine: 0.32</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Levitated_dipole" title="Levitated dipole">Levitated dipole</a> experiment:<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> 0.26</li>
<li>Spheromaks: ≈ 0.1,<sup id="cite_ref-WHAT_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHAT-52">[52]</a></sup> Maximum 0.2 based on Mercier limit.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/DIII-D" class="mw-redirect" title="DIII-D">DIII-D</a> machine: 0.126<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gas_Dynamic_Trap" title="Gas Dynamic Trap">Gas Dynamic Trap</a> a magnetic mirror: 0.6 <sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> for 5E-3 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-Present_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Present-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p><i>Magnetic Mirror</i> One example of magnetic confinement is with the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">magnetic mirror</a> effect. If a particle follows the field line and enters a region of higher field strength, the particles can be reflected. There are several devices that try to use this effect. The most famous was the magnetic mirror machines, which was a series of large, expensive devices built at the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> from the 1960s to mid 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Some other examples include the magnetic bottles and <a href="/wiki/Biconic_cusp" title="Biconic cusp">Biconic cusp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Because the mirror machines were straight, they had some advantages over a ring shape. First, mirrors were easier to construct and maintain and second <a href="/wiki/Direct_energy_conversion" title="Direct energy conversion">direct conversion</a> energy capture, was easier to implement.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">[13]</a></sup> As the confinement achieved in experiments was poor, this approach was abandoned.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><i>Magnetic Loops</i> Another example of magnetic confinement is to bend the field lines back on themselves, either in circles or more commonly in nested <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">toroidal</a> surfaces. The most highly developed system of this type is the <i><a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a></i>, with the <i><a href="/wiki/Stellarator" title="Stellarator">stellarator</a></i> being next most advanced, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Reversed_field_pinch" title="Reversed field pinch">Reversed field pinch</a>. <a href="/wiki/Compact_toroid" title="Compact toroid">Compact toroids</a>, especially the <i><a href="/wiki/Field-Reversed_Configuration" class="mw-redirect" title="Field-Reversed Configuration">Field-Reversed Configuration</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Spheromak" title="Spheromak">spheromak</a>, attempt to combine the advantages of toroidal magnetic surfaces with those of a <a href="/wiki/Simply_connected_space" title="Simply connected space">simply connected</a> (non-toroidal) machine, resulting in a mechanically simpler and smaller confinement area.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_confinement_2">Inertial confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Inertial confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Inertial confinement</a> is the use of rapidly imploding shell to heat and confine plasma. The shell is imploded using a direct laser blast (direct drive) or a secondary x-ray blast (indirect drive) or heavy ion beams. Theoretically, fusion using lasers would be done using tiny pellets of fuel that explode several times a second. To induce the explosion, the pellet must be compressed to about 30 times solid density with energetic beams. If direct drive is used—the beams are focused directly on the pellet—it can in principle be very efficient, but in practice is difficult to obtain the needed uniformity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPfalzner200619.E2.80.9320_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPfalzner200619.E2.80.9320-58">[58]</a></sup> The alternative approach, indirect drive, uses beams to heat a shell, and then the shell radiates <a href="/wiki/X-rays" class="mw-redirect" title="X-rays">x-rays</a>, which then implode the pellet. The beams are commonly laser beams, but heavy and light <a href="/wiki/Ion_beam" title="Ion beam">ion beams</a> and electron beams have all been investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPfalzner2006p182.E2.80.93193_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPfalzner2006p182.E2.80.93193-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrostatic_confinement">Electrostatic confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Electrostatic confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are also <a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">electrostatic confinement fusion</a> devices. These devices confine <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> using electrostatic fields. The best known is the <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">Fusor</a>. This device has a cathode inside an anode wire cage. Positive ions fly towards the negative inner cage, and are heated by the electric field in the process. If they miss the inner cage they can collide and fuse. Ions typically hit the cathode, however, creating prohibitory high <a href="/wiki/Conduction_(heat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conduction (heat)">conduction</a> losses. Also, fusion rates in <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusors</a> are very low because of competing physical effects, such as energy loss in the form of light radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-Thorson1996_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thorson1996-60">[60]</a></sup> Designs have been proposed to avoid the problems associated with the cage, by generating the field using a non-neutral cloud. These include a plasma oscillating device,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1510.01788">magnetically-shielded-grid</a> a <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">penning trap</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">polywell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The technology is relatively immature, however, and many scientific and engineering questions remain.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_research">History of research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History of research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_nuclear_fusion" title="Timeline of nuclear fusion">Timeline of nuclear fusion</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1920s">1920s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 1920s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Research into nuclear fusion started in the early part of the 20th century. In 1920 the British physicist <a href="/wiki/Francis_William_Aston" title="Francis William Aston">Francis William Aston</a> discovered that the total mass equivalent of four <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atoms</a> (two protons and two neutrons) are heavier than the total mass of one <a href="/wiki/Helium_atom" title="Helium atom">helium atom</a> (<a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">He-4</a>), which implied that net energy can be released by combining hydrogen atoms together to form helium, and provided the first hints of a mechanism by which stars could produce energy in the quantities being measured. Through the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Stanley_Eddington" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Stanley Eddington">Arthur Stanley Eddington</a> became a major proponent of the <a href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton–proton chain reaction">proton–proton chain reaction</a> (PP reaction) as the primary system running the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1930s">1930s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 1930s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A theory was verified by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> in 1939 showing that <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum tunneling">quantum tunneling</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">Sun's core</a> might convert one of the protons into a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> and thereby producing <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> rather than a diproton. The deuterium would then fuse through other reactions to further increase the energy output. For this work, Bethe won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1940s">1940s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1940s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1942, nuclear fusion research was subsumed into the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Secrecy" title="Secrecy">secrecy</a> surrounding the field obscured by the science. The first patent related to a fusion reactor was registered in 1946<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Atomic_Energy_Authority" title="United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority">United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority</a>. The inventors were <a href="/wiki/George_Paget_Thomson" title="George Paget Thomson">Sir George Paget Thomson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moses_Blackman" title="Moses Blackman">Moses Blackman</a>. This was the first detailed examination of the <a href="/wiki/Z-pinch" title="Z-pinch">Z-pinch</a> concept.</p>
<p>Z-pinch is based on the fact that plasmas are electrically conducting. Running a current through the plasma, will generate a magnetic field around the plasma. This field will, according to <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz's law">Lenz's law</a>, create an inward directed force that causes the plasma to collapse inward, raising its density. Denser plasmas generate denser magnetic fields, increasing the inward force, leading to a <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reaction</a>. If the conditions are correct, this can lead to the densities and temperatures needed for fusion. The difficulty is getting the current into the plasma, which would normally melt any sort of mechanical <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrode</a>. A solution emerges again because of the conducting nature of the plasma; by placing the plasma in the middle of an <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">induction</a> can be used to generate the current.</p>
<p>Starting in 1947, two UK teams carried out small experiments and began building a series of ever-larger experiments. When the Huemul results hit the news (see below), <a href="/wiki/James_L._Tuck" title="James L. Tuck">James L. Tuck</a>, a UK physicist working at Los Alamos, introduced the pinch concept in the US and produced a series of machines known as the <a href="/wiki/Perhapsatron" title="Perhapsatron">Perhapsatron</a>. The Soviet Union, unbeknownst to the West, was also building a series of similar machines. All of these devices quickly demonstrated a series of instabilities when the pinch was applied. This broke up the plasma column long before it reached the densities and temperatures required for fusion.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1950s">1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IvyMike2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/300px-IvyMike2.jpg" width="300" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/450px-IvyMike2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/600px-IvyMike2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1470" data-file-height="1098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IvyMike2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first man-made device to achieve <a href="/wiki/Fusion_gain_factor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fusion gain factor">ignition</a> was the detonation of this fusion device, codenamed <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg/300px-Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg/450px-Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg/600px-Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="685" data-file-height="456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kink_instability_at_Aldermaston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Early photo of plasma inside a pinch machine (imperial college 1950/1951)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first successful man-made fusion device was the <a href="/wiki/Boosted_fission_weapon" title="Boosted fission weapon">boosted fission weapon</a> tested in 1951 in the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Item" title="Greenhouse Item">Greenhouse Item</a> test. This was followed by true fusion weapons in 1952's <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a>, and the first practical examples in 1954's <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bravo" title="Castle Bravo">Castle Bravo</a>. This was uncontrolled fusion. In these devices, the energy released by the fission explosion is used to compress and heat fusion fuel, starting a fusion reaction. Fusion releases <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> hit the surrounding fission fuel, causing the atoms to split apart much faster than normal fission processes—almost instantly by comparison. This increases the effectiveness of bombs: normal fission weapons blow themselves apart before all their fuel is used; fusion/fission weapons do not have this practical upper limit.</p>
<p>In 1949 an expatriate German, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Richter" title="Ronald Richter">Ronald Richter</a>, proposed the <a href="/wiki/Huemul_Project" title="Huemul Project">Huemul Project</a> in Argentina, announcing positive results in 1951. These turned out to be fake, but it prompted considerable interest in the concept as a whole. In particular, it prompted <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer" title="Lyman Spitzer">Lyman Spitzer</a> to begin considering ways to solve some of the more obvious problems involved in confining a hot plasma, and, unaware of the z-pinch efforts, he developed a new solution to the problem known as the <a href="/wiki/Stellarator" title="Stellarator">stellarator</a>. Spitzer applied to the US <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> for funding to build a test device. During this period, <a href="/wiki/James_L._Tuck" title="James L. Tuck">Jim Tuck</a> who had worked with the UK teams had been introducing the z-pinch concept to his coworkers at his new job at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> (LANL). When he heard of Spitzer's pitch for funding, he applied to build a machine of his own, the <a href="/wiki/Perhapsatron" title="Perhapsatron">Perhapsatron</a>.</p>
<p>Spitzer's idea won funding and he began work on the stellarator under the code name Project Matterhorn. His work led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Plasma_Physics_Laboratory" title="Princeton Plasma Physics Laboratory">Princeton Plasma Physics Laboratory</a>. Tuck returned to LANL and arranged local funding to build his machine. By this time, however, it was clear that all of the pinch machines were suffering from the same issues involving stability, and progress stalled. In 1953, Tuck and others suggested a number of solutions to the stability problems. This led to the design of a second series of pinch machines, led by the UK <a href="/wiki/ZETA_(fusion_reactor)" title="ZETA (fusion reactor)">ZETA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sceptre_(fusion_reactor)" title="Sceptre (fusion reactor)">Sceptre</a> devices.</p>
<p>Spitzer had planned an aggressive development project of four machines, A, B, C, and D. A and B were small research devices, C would be the prototype of a power-producing machine, and D would be the prototype of a commercial device. A worked without issue, but even by the time B was being used it was clear the stellarator was also suffering from instabilities and plasma leakage. Progress on C slowed as attempts were made to correct for these problems.</p>
<p>By the mid-1950s it was clear that the simple theoretical tools being used to calculate the performance of all fusion machines were simply not predicting their actual behavior. Machines invariably leaked their plasma from their confinement area at rates far higher than predicted. In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Teller" title="Edward Teller">Edward Teller</a> held a gathering of fusion researchers at the Princeton Gun Club, near the Project Matterhorn (now known as <a href="/wiki/Project_Sherwood" title="Project Sherwood">Project Sherwood</a>) grounds. Teller started by pointing out the problems that everyone was having, and suggested that any system where the plasma was confined within concave fields was doomed to fail. Attendees remember him saying something to the effect that the fields were like rubber bands, and they would attempt to snap back to a straight configuration whenever the power was increased, ejecting the plasma. He went on to say that it appeared the only way to confine the plasma in a stable configuration would be to use convex fields, a "cusp" configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the meeting concluded, most of the researchers quickly turned out papers saying why Teller's concerns did not apply to their particular device. The pinch machines did not use magnetic fields in this way at all, while the mirror and stellarator seemed to have various ways out. This was soon followed by a paper by <a href="/wiki/Martin_David_Kruskal" title="Martin David Kruskal">Martin David Kruskal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Schwarzschild" title="Martin Schwarzschild">Martin Schwarzschild</a> discussing pinch machines, however, which demonstrated instabilities in those devices were inherent to the design.</p>
<p>The largest "classic" pinch device was the <a href="/wiki/ZETA_(fusion_reactor)" title="ZETA (fusion reactor)">ZETA</a>, including all of these suggested upgrades, starting operations in the UK in 1957. In early 1958, <a href="/wiki/John_Cockcroft" title="John Cockcroft">John Cockcroft</a> announced that fusion had been achieved in the ZETA, an announcement that made headlines around the world. When physicists in the US expressed concerns about the claims they were initially dismissed. US experiments soon demonstrated the same neutrons, although temperature measurements suggested these could not be from fusion reactions. The neutrons seen in the UK were later demonstrated to be from different versions of the same instability processes that plagued earlier machines. Cockcroft was forced to retract the fusion claims, and the entire field was tainted for years. ZETA ended its experiments in 1968.</p>
<p>The first controlled fusion experiment was accomplished using Scylla I at the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> in 1958. This was a pinch machine, with a cylinder full of deuterium. Electric current shot down the sides of the cylinder. The current made magnetic fields that compressed the plasma to 15 million degrees Celsius, squeezed the gas, fused it and produced neutrons.<sup id="cite_ref-Seife.2C_Charles_2008_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seife.2C_Charles_2008-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Phillips.2C_James_2013_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips.2C_James_2013-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1950–1951 <a href="/wiki/Igor_Tamm" title="Igor Tamm">I.E. Tamm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov" title="Andrei Sakharov">A.D. Sakharov</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, first discussed a <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a>-like approach. Experimental research on those designs began in 1956 at the <a href="/wiki/Kurchatov_Institute" title="Kurchatov Institute">Kurchatov Institute</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> by a group of Soviet scientists led by <a href="/wiki/Lev_Artsimovich" title="Lev Artsimovich">Lev Artsimovich</a>. The tokamak essentially combined a low-power pinch device with a low-power simple stellarator. The key was to combine the fields in such a way that the particles orbited within the reactor a particular number of times, today known as the "<a href="/wiki/Safety_factor_(plasma_physics)" title="Safety factor (plasma physics)">safety factor</a>". The combination of these fields dramatically improved confinement times and densities, resulting in huge improvements over existing devices.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A key plasma physics text was published by <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer" title="Lyman Spitzer">Lyman Spitzer</a> at Princeton in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Spitzer took the ideal gas laws and adopted them to an ionized plasma, developing many of the fundamental equations used to model a plasma.</p>
<p>Laser fusion was suggested in 1962 by scientists at <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a>, shortly after the invention of the laser itself in 1960. At the time, Lasers were low power machines, but low-level research began as early as 1965. Laser fusion, formally known as <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement fusion</a>, involves <a href="/wiki/Implosion_(mechanical_process)" title="Implosion (mechanical process)">imploding</a> a target by using <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> beams. There are two ways to do this: indirect drive and direct drive. In direct drive, the laser blasts a pellet of fuel. In indirect drive, the lasers blast a structure around the fuel. This makes <a href="/wiki/X-rays" class="mw-redirect" title="X-rays">x-rays</a> that squeeze the fuel. Both methods compress the fuel so that fusion can take place.</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1964_World%27s_Fair" class="mw-redirect" title="1964 World's Fair">1964 World's Fair</a>, the public was given its first demonstration of nuclear fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The device was a θ-pinch from General Electric. This was similar to the Scylla machine developed earlier at Los Alamos.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">magnetic mirror</a> was first published in 1967 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Post" title="Richard F. Post">Richard F. Post</a> and many others at the Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Mirror_Systems_1969_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirror_Systems_1969-17">[17]</a></sup> The mirror consisted of two large magnets arranged so they had strong fields within them, and a weaker, but connected, field between them. Plasma introduced in the area between the two magnets would "bounce back" from the stronger fields in the middle.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov" title="Andrei Sakharov">A.D. Sakharov</a> group constructed the first tokamaks, the most successful being the <a href="/w/index.php?title=T-3_(tokamak)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="T-3 (tokamak) (page does not exist)">T-3</a> and its larger version <a href="/w/index.php?title=T-4_(tokamak)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="T-4 (tokamak) (page does not exist)">T-4</a>. T-4 was tested in 1968 in <a href="/wiki/Novosibirsk" title="Novosibirsk">Novosibirsk</a>, producing the world's first quasistationary fusion reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> When this were first announced, the international community was highly skeptical. A British team was invited to see T-3, however, and after measuring it in depth they released their results that confirmed the Soviet claims. A burst of activity followed as many planned devices were abandoned and new tokamaks were introduced in their place — the C model stellarator, then under construction after many redesigns, was quickly converted to the Symmetrical Tokamak.</p>
<p>In his work with vacuum tubes, <a href="/wiki/Philo_Farnsworth" title="Philo Farnsworth">Philo Farnsworth</a> observed that electric charge would accumulate in regions of the tube. Today, this effect is known as the <a href="/wiki/Multipactor_effect" title="Multipactor effect">Multipactor effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Farnsworth reasoned that if ions were concentrated high enough they could collide and fuse. In 1962, he filed a patent on a design using a positive inner cage to concentrate plasma, in order to achieve nuclear fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> During this time, <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Hirsch" title="Robert L. Hirsch">Robert L. Hirsch</a> joined the Farnsworth Television labs and began work on what became the fusor. Hirsch patented the design in 1966<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and published the design in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:448px;max-width:448px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg/220px-Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg" width="220" height="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg/330px-Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg/440px-Shiva_amplifier_chains.jpg 2x" data-file-width="776" data-file-height="775" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Shiva laser, 1977, the largest ICF laser system built in the seventies</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg/220px-The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg" width="220" height="164" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg/330px-The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg/440px-The_Tandem_Mirror_Experiment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="930" data-file-height="695" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Tandem Mirror Experiment (TMX) in 1979</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1972, John Nuckolls outlined the idea of ignition.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuckolls.2C_John_1972_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuckolls.2C_John_1972-21">[21]</a></sup> This is a fusion chain reaction. Hot helium made during fusion reheats the fuel and starts more reactions. John argued that ignition would require lasers of about 1&#160;kJ. This turned out to be wrong. Nuckolls's paper started a major development effort. Several laser systems were built at LLNL. These included the <a href="/wiki/Argus_laser" title="Argus laser">argus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops_laser" title="Cyclops laser">Cyclops</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Janus_laser" title="Janus laser">Janus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Long_path_laser" title="Long path laser">long path</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Shiva_laser" title="Shiva laser">Shiva laser</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nova_(laser)" title="Nova (laser)">Nova</a> in 1984. This prompted the UK to build the <a href="/wiki/Central_Laser_Facility" title="Central Laser Facility">Central Laser Facility</a> in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this time, great strides in understanding the tokamak system were made. A number of improvements to the design are now part of the "advanced tokamak" concept, which includes non-circular plasma, internal diverters and limiters, often superconducting magnets, and operate in the so-called "H-mode" island of increased stability.
