Two other designs have also become fairly well studied; the compact tokamak is wired with the magnets on the inside of the vacuum chamber, while the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_tokamak" title="Spherical tokamak">spherical tokamak</a> reduces its cross section as much as possible.</p>
<p>In 1974 a study of the ZETA results demonstrated an interesting side-effect; after an experimental run ended, the plasma would enter a short period of stability. This led to the <a href="/wiki/Reversed_field_pinch" title="Reversed field pinch">reversed field pinch</a> concept, which has seen some level of development since. On May 1, 1974, the KMS fusion company (founded by <a href="/wiki/Kip_Siegel" title="Kip Siegel">Kip Siegel</a>) achieves the world's first laser induced fusion in a deuterium-tritium pellet.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1970s, <a href="/wiki/Project_PACER" title="Project PACER">Project PACER</a>, carried out at Los Alamos National Laboratory (LANL) explored the possibility of a fusion power system that would involve exploding small <a href="/wiki/H-bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="H-bomb">hydrogen bombs</a> (fusion bombs) inside an underground cavity.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> As an energy source, the system is the only fusion power system that could be demonstrated to work using existing technology. It would also require a large, continuous supply of nuclear bombs, however, making the economics of such a system rather questionable.</p>
<p>In 1976, the two beam <a href="/wiki/Argus_laser" title="Argus laser">Argus laser</a> becomes operational at livermore<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. In 1977, The 20 beam <a href="/wiki/Shiva_laser" title="Shiva laser">Shiva laser</a> at Livermore is completed, capable of delivering 10.2 kilojoules of infrared energy on target. At a price of $25 million and a size approaching that of a football field, Shiva is the first of the megalasers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. That same year, the <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">JET</a> project is approved by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> and a site is selected.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg/180px-TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg" width="180" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg/270px-TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg/360px-TMX_Baseball_Coils.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1032" data-file-height="769" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Magnetic mirrors suffered from end losses, requiring high power, complex magnetic designs, such as the baseball coil pictured here.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg/180px-The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg" width="180" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg/270px-The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg/360px-The_Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility_During_Construction.jpg 2x" data-file-width="369" data-file-height="270" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The magnetic mirror test facility during construction</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Novette_laser.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Novette_laser.jpg/200px-Novette_laser.jpg" width="200" height="366" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Novette_laser.jpg/300px-Novette_laser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Novette_laser.jpg/400px-Novette_laser.jpg 2x" data-file-width="789" data-file-height="1443" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Novette target chamber (metal sphere with diagnostic devices protruding radially), which was reused from the <a href="/wiki/Shiva_laser" title="Shiva laser">Shiva</a> project and two newly built laser chains visible in background.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg/200px-Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg" width="200" height="297" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg/300px-Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Fusion_target_implosion_on_NOVA_laser.jpg 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="587" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Inertial confinement fusion implosion on the <a href="/wiki/Nova_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="Nova laser">Nova laser</a> during the 1980s was a key driver of fusion development.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As a result of advocacy, the cold war, and the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">1970s energy crisis</a> a massive <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mirror" title="Magnetic mirror">magnetic mirror</a> program was funded by the US federal government in the late 1970s and early 1980s. This program resulted in a series of large magnetic mirror devices including: 2X,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Baseball I, Baseball II, the <a href="/wiki/Tandem_Mirror_Experiment" title="Tandem Mirror Experiment">Tandem Mirror Experiment</a>, the Tandem mirror experiment upgrade, the <a href="/wiki/Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility" title="Mirror Fusion Test Facility">Mirror Fusion Test Facility</a> and the MFTF-B. These machines were built and tested at Livermore from the late 1960s to the mid 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> A number of institutions collaborated on these machines, conducting experiments. These included the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Institute for Advanced Study">Institute for Advanced Study</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a>. The last machine, the <a href="/wiki/Mirror_Fusion_Test_Facility" title="Mirror Fusion Test Facility">Mirror Fusion Test Facility</a> cost 372 million dollars and was, at that time, the most expensive project in Livermore history.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> It opened on February 21, 1986 and was promptly shut down. The reason given was to balance the United States federal budget. This program was supported from within the Carter and early Reagan administrations by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_E._Kintner" title="Edwin E. Kintner">Edwin E. Kintner</a>, a US Navy captain, under <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Trivelpiece" title="Alvin Trivelpiece">Alvin Trivelpiece</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Laser fusion progressed: in 1983, the <a href="/wiki/NOVETTE_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="NOVETTE laser">NOVETTE laser</a> was completed. The following December 1984, the ten beam <a href="/wiki/NOVA_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="NOVA laser">NOVA laser</a> was finished. Five years later, NOVA would produce a maximum of 120 kilojoules of infrared light, during a nanosecond pulse<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Meanwhile, efforts focused on either fast delivery or beam smoothness. Both tried to deliver the energy uniformly to implode the target. One early problem was that the light in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> wavelength, lost lots of energy before hitting the fuel. Breakthroughs were made at the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_for_Laser_Energetics" title="Laboratory for Laser Energetics">Laboratory for Laser Energetics</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a>. Rochester scientists used frequency-tripling crystals to transform the infrared laser beams into ultraviolet beams. In 1985, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Donna_Strickland&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Donna Strickland (page does not exist)">Donna Strickland</a><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Mourou" title="Gérard Mourou">Gérard Mourou</a> invented a method to amplify lasers pulses by "chirping". This method changes a single wavelength into a full spectrum. The system then amplifies the laser at each wavelength and then reconstitutes the beam into one color. Chirp pulsed amplification became instrumental in building the National Ignition Facility and the Omega EP system. Most research into ICF was towards weapons research, because the implosion is relevant to nuclear weapons.</p>
<p>During this time <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> constructed a series of laser facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-NRDC_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRDC-81">[81]</a></sup> This included Gemini (a two beam system), Helios (eight beams), Antares (24 beams) and Aurora (96 beams).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The program ended in the early nineties with a cost on the order of one billion dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-NRDC_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRDC-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1987, Akira Hasegawa <sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> noticed that in a dipolar magnetic field, fluctuations tended compress the plasma without energy loss. This effect was noticed in data taken by <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a>, when it encountered Uranus. This observation would become the basis for a fusion approach known as the <a href="/wiki/Levitated_dipole" title="Levitated dipole">Levitated dipole</a>.</p>
<p>In Tokamaks, the <a href="/wiki/Tore_Supra" title="Tore Supra">Tore Supra</a> was under construction over the middle of the eighties (1983 to 1988). This was a <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">Tokamak</a> built in <a href="/wiki/Cadarache" title="Cadarache">Cadarache</a>, France.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">JET</a> was completed and first plasmas achieved. In 1985, the Japanese tokamak, <a href="/wiki/JT-60" title="JT-60">JT-60</a> was completed. In 1988, the <a href="/wiki/T-15_(reactor)" title="T-15 (reactor)">T-15</a> a Soviet tokamak was completed. It was the first industrial fusion reactor to use <a href="/wiki/Superconducting" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconducting">superconducting</a> magnets to control the plasma. These were Helium cooled.</p>
<p>In 1989, Pons and Fleischmann submitted papers to the <i>Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry</i> claiming that they had observed fusion in a room temperature device and disclosing their work in a press release.<sup id="cite_ref-Utahpress1989_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utahpress1989-86">[86]</a></sup> Some scientists reported excess heat, neutrons, tritium, helium and other nuclear effects in so-called <a href="/wiki/Cold_fusion" title="Cold fusion">cold fusion</a> systems, which for a time gained interest as showing promise. Hopes fell when replication failures were weighed in view of several reasons cold fusion is not likely to occur, the discovery of possible sources of experimental error, and finally the discovery that Fleischmann and Pons had not actually detected nuclear reaction byproducts.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> By late 1989, most scientists considered cold fusion claims dead,<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_1989_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne_1989-88">[88]</a></sup> and cold fusion subsequently gained a reputation as <a href="/wiki/Pathological_science" title="Pathological science">pathological science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytdoe_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytdoe-89">[89]</a></sup> However, a small community of researchers continues to investigate cold fusion<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_1989_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne_1989-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytscorn_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytscorn-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wired_march_2009_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired_march_2009-92">[92]</a></sup> claiming to replicate Fleishmann and Pons' results including nuclear reaction byproducts.<sup id="cite_ref-ACS_Press_Release_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACS_Press_Release-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hagelstein_et_al._2004_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hagelstein_et_al._2004-94">[94]</a></sup> Claims related to cold fusion are largely disbelieved in the mainstream scientific community.<sup id="cite_ref-Feder_2005_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feder_2005-95">[95]</a></sup> In 1989, the majority of a review panel organized by the <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Energy">US Department of Energy</a> (DOE) found that the evidence for the discovery of a new nuclear process was not persuasive. A second DOE review, convened in 2004 to look at new research, reached conclusions similar to the first.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1984, Martin Peng of ORNL proposed<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> an alternate arrangement of the magnet coils that would greatly reduce the aspect ratio while avoiding the erosion issues of the compact tokamak: a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_tokamak" title="Spherical tokamak">Spherical tokamak</a>. Instead of wiring each magnet coil separately, he proposed using a single large conductor in the center, and wiring the magnets as half-rings off of this conductor. What was once a series of individual rings passing through the hole in the center of the reactor was reduced to a single post, allowing for aspect ratios as low as 1.2.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Braams_and_Scott.2C_pg._225_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Braams_and_Scott.2C_pg._225-99">[99]</a></sup> The ST concept appeared to represent an enormous advance in tokamak design. However, it was being proposed during a period when US fusion research budgets were being dramatically scaled back. ORNL was provided with funds to develop a suitable central column built out of a high-strength copper alloy called "Glidcop". However, they were unable to secure funding to build a demonstration machine, "STX". Failing to build an ST at ORNL, Peng began a worldwide effort to interest other teams in the ST concept and get a test machine built. One way to do this quickly would be to convert a spheromak machine to the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_tokamak" title="Spherical tokamak">Spherical tokamak</a> layout.<sup id="cite_ref-Braams_and_Scott.2C_pg._225_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Braams_and_Scott.2C_pg._225-99">[99]</a></sup> Peng's advocacy also caught the interest of <a href="/wiki/Derek_Robinson_(physicist)" title="Derek Robinson (physicist)">Derek Robinson</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Atomic_Energy_Authority" title="United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority">United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority</a> fusion center at <a href="/wiki/Culham" title="Culham">Culham</a>. Robinson was able to gather together a team and secure funding on the order of 100,000 pounds to build an experimental machine, the <a href="/wiki/Small_Tight_Aspect_Ratio_Tokamak" title="Small Tight Aspect Ratio Tokamak">Small Tight Aspect Ratio Tokamak</a>, or START. Several parts of the machine were recycled from earlier projects, while others were loaned from other labs, including a 40 keV neutral beam injector from ORNL. Construction of <a href="/wiki/Small_Tight_Aspect_Ratio_Tokamak" title="Small Tight Aspect Ratio Tokamak">START</a> began in 1990, it was assembled rapidly and started operation in January 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Z-machine480.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/Z-machine480.jpg/220px-Z-machine480.jpg" width="220" height="146" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/Z-machine480.jpg/330px-Z-machine480.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/0b/Z-machine480.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="291" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Z Machine (a pinch at SNL) went through a number of upgrades during the mid to late nineties</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Nif_hohlraum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nif_hohlraum.jpg/140px-Nif_hohlraum.jpg" width="140" height="175" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nif_hohlraum.jpg/210px-Nif_hohlraum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nif_hohlraum.jpg/280px-Nif_hohlraum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1750" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Mockup of a gold-plated hohlraum designed for use in the <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">National Ignition Facility</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1991 the Preliminary Tritium Experiment at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">Joint European Torus</a> in England achieved the world’s first controlled release of fusion power.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992, a major article was published in Physics Today by Robert McCory at the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_for_laser_energetics" class="mw-redirect" title="Laboratory for laser energetics">Laboratory for laser energetics</a> outlying the current state of ICF and advocating for a national ignition facility.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> This was followed up by a major review article, from John Lindl in 1995,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> advocating for <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">NIF</a>. During this time a number of ICF subsystems were developing, including target manufacturing, cryogenic handling systems, new laser designs (notably the <a href="/wiki/Nike_laser" title="Nike laser">NIKE</a> laser at <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Research_Laboratory" title="United States Naval Research Laboratory">NRL</a>) and improved diagnostics like time of flight analyzers and <a href="/wiki/Thomson_scattering" title="Thomson scattering">Thomson scattering</a>. This work was done at the <a href="/wiki/Nova_(laser)" title="Nova (laser)">NOVA</a> laser system, <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics" title="General Atomics">General Atomics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser_M%C3%A9gajoule" title="Laser Mégajoule">Laser Mégajoule</a> and the <a href="/wiki/GEKKO_XII" title="GEKKO XII">GEKKO XII</a> system in Japan. Through this work and lobbying by groups like the fusion power associates and John Sethian at NRL, a vote was made in congress, authorizing funding for the NIF project in the late nineties.</p>
<p>In the early nineties, theory and experimental work regarding fusors and polywells was published.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In response, Todd Rider at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> developed general models of these devices.<sup id="cite_ref-Plasma_Physics_1995_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plasma_Physics_1995-106">[106]</a></sup> Rider argued that all plasma systems at thermodynamic equilibrium were fundamentally limited. In 1995, William Nevins published a criticism <sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> arguing that the particles inside fusors and polywells would build up <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, causing the dense core to degrade.</p>
<p>In 1995, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a> built a large <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a>, known as HOMER, which is still in operation.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Dr <a href="/wiki/George_H._Miley" title="George H. Miley">George H. Miley</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana%E2%80%93Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign">Illinois</a>, built a small fusor that has produced neutrons using deuterium gas <sup id="cite_ref-Physics_Research_1999_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Physics_Research_1999-109">[109]</a></sup> and discovered the "star mode" of fusor operation.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The following year, the first "US-Japan Workshop on IEC Fusion", was conducted. At this time in Europe, an IEC device was developed as a commercial neutron source by <a href="/wiki/DaimlerChrysler_Aerospace" class="mw-redirect" title="DaimlerChrysler Aerospace">Daimler-Chrysler</a> and NSD Fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, the Z-machine was upgraded and opened to the public by the US Army in August 1998 in Scientific American.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The key attributes of Sandia’s Z machine<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> are its 18 million amperes and a discharge time of less than 100 <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">nanoseconds</a>. This generates a magnetic pulse, inside a large oil tank, this strikes an array of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> wires called a <i>liner</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Firing the Z-machine has become a way to test very high energy, high temperature (2 billion degrees) conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1996, the <a href="/wiki/Tore_Supra" title="Tore Supra">Tore Supra</a> creates a plasma for two minutes with a current of almost 1 million amperes driven non-inductively by 2.3 MW of <a href="/wiki/Lower_hybrid_oscillation" title="Lower hybrid oscillation">lower hybrid frequency waves</a>. This is 280 MJ of injected and extracted energy. This result was possible because of the actively cooled plasma-facing components<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1997, JET produced a peak of 16.1MW of fusion power (65% of heat to plasma<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>), with fusion power of over 10MW sustained for over 0.5&#160;sec. Its successor, the International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor (<a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a>), was officially announced as part of a seven-party consortium (six countries and the EU).<sup id="cite_ref-announcement_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-announcement-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> is designed to produce ten times more fusion power than the power put into the <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)#Artificial_plasmas" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>. <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> is currently under construction in <a href="/wiki/Cadarache" title="Cadarache">Cadarache</a>, France.</p>
<p>In the late nineties, a team at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Levitated_dipole" title="Levitated dipole">Levitated dipole</a> a fusion device which consisted of a superconducting electromagnet, floating in a saucer shaped vacuum chamber. Plasma swirled around this donut and fused along the center axis.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG/220px-Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG" width="220" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG/330px-Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG/440px-Homemade_fusion_reactor.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Starting in 1999, a growing number of amateurs have been able to fuse atoms using homemade <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusors</a>, shown here.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MAST_plasma_image.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/MAST_plasma_image.jpg/220px-MAST_plasma_image.jpg" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b2/MAST_plasma_image.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="231" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MAST_plasma_image.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Mega Ampere Spherical Tokamak became operational in the UK in 1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the March 8, 2002 issue of the peer-reviewed journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science magazine">Science</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Rusi_Taleyarkhan" title="Rusi Taleyarkhan">Rusi P. Taleyarkhan</a> and colleagues at the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory (ORNL)</a> reported that acoustic cavitation experiments conducted with <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterated</a> <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a><sub>3</sub><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">D</a><sub>6</sub><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a>) showed measurements of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> output consistent with the occurrence of fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-Taleyarkhan-2002-03-08_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taleyarkhan-2002-03-08-125">[125]</a></sup> Taleyarkhan was later found guilty of misconduct,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Research" title="Office of Naval Research">Office of Naval Research</a> debarred him for 28 months from receiving Federal Funding,<sup id="cite_ref-reich_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reich-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and his name was listed in the 'Excluded Parties List'.<sup id="cite_ref-reich_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reich-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Fast ignition" was developed in the late nineties, and was part of a push by the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_for_Laser_Energetics" title="Laboratory for Laser Energetics">Laboratory for Laser Energetics</a> for building the Omega EP system. This system was finished in 2008. Fast ignition showed such dramatic power savings that ICF appears to be a useful technique for energy production. There are even proposals to build an experimental facility dedicated to the fast ignition approach, known as <a href="/wiki/HiPER" title="HiPER">HiPER</a>.</p>
<p>In April 2005, a team from <a href="/wiki/UCLA" class="mw-redirect" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> announced<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> it had devised a way of producing fusion using a machine that "fits on a lab bench", using <a href="/wiki/Lithium_tantalate" title="Lithium tantalate">lithium tantalate</a> to generate enough voltage to smash deuterium atoms together. The process, however, does not generate net power (see <a href="/wiki/Pyroelectric_fusion" title="Pyroelectric fusion">Pyroelectric fusion</a>). Such a device would be useful in the same sort of roles as the fusor. In 2006, China's <a href="/wiki/EAST" class="mw-redirect" title="EAST">EAST</a> test reactor is completed. This was the first tokamak to use superconducting magnets to generate both the toroidal and poloidal fields.</p>
<p>In the early 2000s, Researchers at <a href="/wiki/LANL" class="mw-redirect" title="LANL">LANL</a> reasoned that a plasma oscillating could be at local thermodynamic equilibrium. This prompted the POPS and <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">Penning trap</a> designs.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-131">[131]</a></sup> At this time, researchers at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> became interested in <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusors</a> for space propulsion<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and powering space vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Specifically, researchers developed <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusors</a> with multiple inner cages. Greg Piefer graduated from Madison and founded <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Nuclear_Labs" title="Phoenix Nuclear Labs">Phoenix Nuclear Labs</a>, a company that developed the <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a> into a neutron source for the mass production of medical isotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-PNL_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNL-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bussard" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Bussard">Robert Bussard</a> began speaking openly about the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">Polywell</a> in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> He attempted to generate interest<sup id="cite_ref-Bussard6_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bussard6-136">[136]</a></sup> in the research, before his death. In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Wilson" title="Taylor Wilson">Taylor Wilson</a> achieved notoriety<sup id="cite_ref-Dutton.2C_Judy_2011_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dutton.2C_Judy_2011-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> for achieving nuclear fusion at 14, with a homemade <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, a high-energy laser system, the <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">National Ignition Facility</a> (NIF), was finished in the US, which can heat hydrogen atoms to temperatures only existing in nature in the cores of stars. The new laser is expected to have the ability to produce, for the first time, more energy from controlled, inertially confined nuclear fusion than was required to initiate the reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg/300px-Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg/450px-Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg/600px-Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5572" data-file-height="3714" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Preamplifier_at_the_National_Ignition_Facility.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The preamplifiers of the National Ignition Facility. In 2012, the NIF achieved a 500-terawatt shot.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg/300px-Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg" width="300" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg/450px-Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg/600px-Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5220" data-file-height="4399" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wendelstein7-X_Torushall-2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Wendelstein7X under construction</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:W7X-Spulen_Plasma_blau_gelb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/W7X-Spulen_Plasma_blau_gelb.jpg/360px-W7X-Spulen_Plasma_blau_gelb.jpg" width="360" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/W7X-Spulen_Plasma_blau_gelb.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:W7X-Spulen_Plasma_blau_gelb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Example of a stellarator design: A coil system (blue) surrounds plasma (yellow). A magnetic field line is highlighted in green on the yellow plasma surface.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2010, NIF researchers were conducting a series of "tuning" shots to determine the optimal target design and laser parameters for high-energy ignition experiments with fusion fuel in the following months.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Two firing tests were performed on October 31, 2010 and November 2, 2010. In early 2012, NIF director Mike Dunne expected the laser system to generate fusion with net energy gain by the end of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> However, it was delayed and not achieved by that date.</p>
<p>Inertial (laser) confinement is being developed at the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">National Ignition Facility</a> (NIF) based at <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Laser_M%C3%A9gajoule" title="Laser Mégajoule">Laser Mégajoule</a>, and the planned <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> <a href="/wiki/HiPER" title="HiPER">High Power laser Energy Research</a> (HiPER) facility. NIF reached initial operational status in 2010 and has been in the process of increasing the power and energy of its "shots", with fusion ignition tests to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-Oct8_10_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oct8_10-145">[145]</a></sup> A three-year goal announced in 2009 to produce net energy from fusion by 2012 was missed; in September 2013, however, the facility announced a significant milestone from an August 2013 test that produced more energy from the fusion reaction than had been provided to the fuel pellet. This was reported as the first time this had been accomplished in fusion power research. The facility reported that their next step involved improving the system to prevent the hohlraum from either breaking up asymmetrically or too soon.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2012 paper demonstrated that a dense plasma focus had achieved temperatures of 1.8 billion degrees Celsius, sufficient for <a href="/wiki/Proton-boron_fusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Proton-boron fusion">boron fusion</a>, and that fusion reactions were occurring primarily within the contained plasmoid, a necessary condition for net power.<sup id="cite_ref-PB11_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PB11-149">[149]</a></sup> The focus consists of two coaxial cylindrical electrodes made from copper or beryllium and housed in a vacuum chamber containing a low-pressure fusible gas. An electrical pulse is applied across the electrodes, heating the gas into a plasma. The current forms into a minuscule vortex along the axis of the machine, which then kinks into a cage of current with an associated magnetic field. The cage of current and magnetic-field-entrapped plasma is called a <a href="/wiki/Plasmoid" title="Plasmoid">plasmoid</a>. The acceleration of the electrons about the magnetic field lines heats the nuclei within the plasmoid to fusion temperatures.</p>
<p>In April 2014, <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> ended the <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Laser Inertial Fusion Energy</a> (LIFE) program and redirected their efforts towards NIF.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In August 2014, <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Nuclear_Labs" title="Phoenix Nuclear Labs">Phoenix Nuclear Labs</a> announced the sale of a high-yield neutron generator that could sustain 5×10<sup>11</sup> <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> fusion reactions per second over a 24-hour period.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In October 2014, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Skunk_Works" title="Skunk Works">Skunk Works</a> announced the development of a <a href="/wiki/High_beta_fusion_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="High beta fusion reactor">high-beta fusion reactor</a> that they hope to yield a functioning 100-megawatt prototype by 2017 and to be ready for regular operation by 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deep_space_exploration" title="Deep space exploration">Deep-space exploration</a>, as well as higher-velocity lower-cost <a href="/wiki/Space_transport" class="mw-redirect" title="Space transport">space transport</a> services in general would be enabled by this <a href="/wiki/Compact_fusion_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact fusion reactor">compact fusion reactor</a> technology.<sup id="cite_ref-aw20141014_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aw20141014-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">polywell</a> was presented at <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Research" title="Microsoft Research">Microsoft Research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August, 2015, <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> announced a <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> it named <a href="/wiki/ARC_fusion_reactor" title="ARC fusion reactor">ARC fusion reactor</a> design using rare-earth barium-copper oxide (REBCO) superconducting tapes to produce high-magnetic field coils that it claimed produce comparable magnetic field strength in a smaller configuration than other designs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2015, researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_of_Plasma_Physics" title="Max Planck Institute of Plasma Physics">Max Planck Institute of Plasma Physics</a> completed building the largest <a href="/wiki/Stellarator" title="Stellarator">stellarator</a> to date, named <a href="/wiki/Wendelstein_7-X" title="Wendelstein 7-X">Wendelstein 7-X</a>. On December 10, they successfully produced the first helium plasma, and on February 3, 2016 produced the device's first Hydrogen plasma.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> With plasma discharges lasting up to 30 minutes, Wendelstein 7-X will try to demonstrate the essential stellarator attribute: continuous operation of a high-temperature hydrogen plasma.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuels">Fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>By firing particle beams at targets, many fusion reactions have been tested, while the fuels considered for power have all been light elements like the isotopes of hydrogen—<a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-osti.gov_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osti.gov-3">[3]</a></sup> Other reactions like the deuterium and Helium<sup>3</sup> reaction or the Helium<sup>3</sup> and Helium<sup>3</sup> reactions, would require a supply of Helium<sup>3</sup>. This can either come from other nuclear reactions or from extraterrestrial sources. Finally, researchers hope to do the p-<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">11<br /></span>B<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> reaction, because it does not directly produce neutrons, though side reactions can.<sup id="cite_ref-AtzeniMeyer-ter-Vehn2004_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AtzeniMeyer-ter-Vehn2004-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deuterium.2C_tritium">Deuterium, tritium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Deuterium, tritium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/220px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/330px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/440px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="248" data-file-height="276" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of the D-T reaction</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The easiest nuclear reaction, at the lowest energy, is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">2<br />
1</span>D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></a></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">3<br />
1</span>T<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></a></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">4<br />
2</span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></a></span> + <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">1<br />
0</span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span></a></dd>
</dl>
<p>This reaction is common in research, industrial and military applications, usually as a convenient source of neutrons. <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> is a naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of hydrogen and is commonly available. The large mass ratio of the hydrogen isotopes makes their separation easy compared to the difficult <a href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" class="mw-redirect" title="Uranium enrichment">uranium enrichment</a> process. <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">Tritium</a> is a natural isotope of hydrogen, but because it has a short <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 12.32 years, it is hard to find, store, produce, and is expensive. Consequently, the deuterium-tritium fuel cycle requires the <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeding</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> using one of the following reactions:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">1<br />
0</span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">6<br />
3</span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">3<br />
1</span>T<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">4<br />
2</span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span></dd>
<dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">1<br />
0</span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">7<br />
3</span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">3<br />
1</span>T<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">4<br />
2</span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">1<br />
0</span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The reactant neutron is supplied by the D-T fusion reaction shown above, and the one that has the greatest yield of energy. The reaction with <sup>6</sup>Li is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic</a>, providing a small energy gain for the reactor. The reaction with <sup>7</sup>Li is <a href="/wiki/Endothermic_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothermic reaction">endothermic</a> but does not consume the neutron. At least some <sup>7</sup>Li reactions are required to replace the neutrons lost to absorption by other elements. Most reactor designs use the naturally occurring mix of lithium isotopes.</p>
<p>Several drawbacks are commonly attributed to D-T fusion power:</p>
<ol>
<li>It produces substantial amounts of neutrons that result in the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of the reactor materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Thinkquest_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thinkquest-160">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li>Only about 20% of the fusion energy yield appears in the form of charged particles with the remainder carried off by neutrons, which limits the extent to which direct energy conversion techniques might be applied.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></li>
<li>It requires the handling of the radioisotope tritium. Similar to hydrogen, tritium is difficult to contain and may leak from reactors in some quantity. Some estimates suggest that this would represent a fairly large environmental release of radioactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Neutron_flux" title="Neutron flux">neutron flux</a> expected in a commercial D-T fusion reactor is about 100 times that of current fission power reactors, posing problems for <a href="/wiki/Plasma_facing_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Plasma facing material">material design</a>. After a series of D-T tests at <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">JET</a>, the vacuum vessel was sufficiently radioactive that remote handling was required for the year following the tests.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a production setting, the neutrons would be used to react with <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> in the context of a breeder blanket comprising lithium ceramic pebbles or liquid lithium, in order to create more tritium. This also deposits the energy of the neutrons in the lithium, which would then be transferred to drive electrical production. The lithium neutron absorption reaction protects the outer portions of the reactor from the neutron flux. Newer designs, the advanced tokamak in particular, also use lithium inside the reactor core as a key element of the design. The plasma interacts directly with the lithium, preventing a problem known as "recycling". The advantage of this design was demonstrated in the <a href="/wiki/Lithium_Tokamak_Experiment" title="Lithium Tokamak Experiment">Lithium Tokamak Experiment</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deuterium">Deuterium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Deuterium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png/220px-Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png/330px-Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png/440px-Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="744" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_Deuterium_Fusion_Cross_Section.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deuterium fusion cross section (in square meters) at different ion collision energies.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This is the second easiest fusion reaction, fusing deuterium with itself. The reaction has two branches that occur with nearly equal probability:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table>
<tr>
<td>D + D</td>
<td>→ T</td>
<td>+ <sup>1</sup>H</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>D + D</td>
<td>→ <sup>3</sup>He</td>
<td>+ n</td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>This reaction is also common in research. The optimum energy to initiate this reaction is 15&#160;keV, only slightly higher than the optimum for the D-T reaction. The first branch does not produce neutrons, but it does produce tritium, so that a D-D reactor will not be completely tritium-free, even though it does not require an input of tritium or lithium. Unless the tritons can be quickly removed, most of the tritium produced would be burned before leaving the reactor, which would reduce the handling of tritium, but would produce more neutrons, some of which are very energetic. The neutron from the second branch has an energy of only 2.45&#160;MeV (0.393&#160;pJ), whereas the neutron from the D-T reaction has an energy of 14.1&#160;MeV (2.26&#160;pJ), resulting in a wider range of isotope production and material damage. When the tritons are removed quickly while allowing the <sup>3</sup>He to react, the fuel cycle is called "tritium suppressed fusion"<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The removed tritium decays to <sup>3</sup>He with a 12.5 year half life. By recycling the <sup>3</sup>He produced from the decay of tritium back into the fusion reactor, the fusion reactor does not require materials resistant to fast 14.1&#160;MeV (2.26&#160;pJ) neutrons.</p>
<p>Assuming complete tritium burn-up, the reduction in the fraction of fusion energy carried by neutrons would be only about 18%, so that the primary advantage of the D-D fuel cycle is that tritium breeding would not be required. Other advantages are independence from scarce<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Fusion_power#Dubious" title="Talk:Fusion power">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> lithium resources and a somewhat softer neutron spectrum. The disadvantage of D-D compared to D-T is that the energy confinement time (at a given pressure) must be 30 times longer and the power produced (at a given pressure and volume) would be 68 times less<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> .</p>
<p>Assuming complete removal of tritium and recycling of <sup>3</sup>He, only 6% of the fusion energy is carried by neutrons. The tritium-suppressed D-D fusion requires an energy confinement that is 10 times longer compared to D-T and a plasma temperature that is twice as high.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deuterium.2C_helium_3">Deuterium, helium 3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Deuterium, helium 3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A second-generation approach to controlled fusion power involves combining <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> (<sup>3</sup>He) and <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> (<sup>2</sup>H):</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table>
<tr>
<td>D + <sup>3</sup>He</td>
<td>→ <sup>4</sup>He</td>
<td>+ <sup>1</sup>H</td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>This reaction produces a helium-4 nucleus (<sup>4</sup>He) and a high-energy proton. As with the p-<sup>11</sup>B <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion">aneutronic fusion</a> fuel cycle, most of the reaction energy is released as charged particles, reducing <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">activation</a> of the reactor housing and potentially allowing more efficient energy harvesting (via any of several speculative technologies).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In practice, D-D side reactions produce a significant number of neutrons, resulting in p-<sup>11</sup>B being the preferred cycle for aneutronic fusion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proton.2C_boron_11">Proton, boron 11</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Proton, boron 11">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion">aneutronic fusion</a> is the goal, then the most promising candidate may be the Hydrogen-1 (proton)/<a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> reaction, which releases alpha (helium) particles, but does not rely on <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">neutron scattering</a> for energy transfer.</p>
<dl>
<dd><sup>1</sup>H + <sup>11</sup>B → 3&#160;<sup>4</sup>He</dd>
</dl>
<p>Under reasonable assumptions, side reactions will result in about 0.1% of the fusion power being carried by neutrons.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> At 123 keV, the optimum temperature for this reaction is nearly ten times higher than that for the pure hydrogen reactions, the energy confinement must be 500 times better than that required for the D-T reaction, and the <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a> will be 2500 times lower than for D-T.</p>
<p>Because the confinement properties of conventional approaches to fusion such as the tokamak and laser pellet fusion are marginal, most proposals for aneutronic fusion are based on radically different confinement concepts, such as the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">Polywell</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dense_Plasma_Focus" class="mw-redirect" title="Dense Plasma Focus">Dense Plasma Focus</a>. Results have been extremely promising:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"In the October 2013 edition of Nature Communications,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> a research team led by Christine Labaune at École Polytechnique in Palaiseau, France, reported a new record fusion rate: an estimated 80 million fusion reactions during the 1.5 nanoseconds that the laser fired, which is at least 100 times more than any previous proton-boron experiment. " <sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Material_selection">Material selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Material selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Fusion_Materials_Irradiation_Facility" title="International Fusion Materials Irradiation Facility">International Fusion Materials Irradiation Facility</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Considerations">Considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Any power station using hot plasma, is going to have plasma facing walls. In even the simplest plasma approaches, the material will get blasted with matter and energy. This leads to a minimum list of considerations, including dealing with:</p>
<ul>
<li>A heating and cooling cycle, up to a 10&#160;MW/m² thermal load.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neutron_radiation" title="Neutron radiation">Neutron radiation</a>, which over time leads to <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embrittlement" title="Embrittlement">embrittlement</a>.</li>
<li>High energy ions leaving at tens to hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electronvolts</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">Alpha particles</a> leaving at millions of <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electronvolts</a>.</li>
<li>Electrons leaving at high energy.</li>
<li>Light radiation (IR, visible, UV, X-ray).</li>
</ul>
<p>Depending on the approach, these effects may be higher or lower than typical <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> reactors like the <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> (PWR).<sup id="cite_ref-Shin_Kajita_2014_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shin_Kajita_2014-169">[169]</a></sup> One estimate put the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_radiation" title="Neutron radiation">radiation</a> at 100 times the (PWR)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Materials need to be selected or developed that can withstand these basic conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Depending on the approach, however, there may be other considerations such as <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">magnetic permeability</a> and mechanical strength. There is also a need for materials whose primary components and impurities do not result in long-lived radioactive wastes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Durability">Durability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Durability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For long term use, each atom in the wall is expected to be hit by a neutron and displaced about a hundred times before the material is replaced. High-energy neutrons will produce hydrogen and helium by way of various nuclear reactions that tends to form bubbles at grain boundaries and result in swelling, blistering or embrittlement.<sup id="cite_ref-Shin_Kajita_2014_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shin_Kajita_2014-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection">Selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One can choose either a low-<a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a> material, such as <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, or a high-<a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a> material, usually <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> with <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> as a second choice. Use of liquid metals (lithium, gallium, tin) has also been proposed, e.g., by injection of 1–5&#160;mm thick streams flowing at 10&#160;m/s on solid substrates<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>If graphite is used, the gross erosion rates due to physical and chemical <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a> would be many meters per year, so one must rely on redeposition of the sputtered material. The location of the redeposition will not exactly coincide with the location of the sputtering, so one is still left with erosion rates that may be prohibitive. An even larger problem is the tritium co-deposited with the redeposited graphite. The tritium inventory in graphite layers and dust in a reactor could quickly build up to many kilograms, representing a waste of resources and a serious radiological hazard in case of an accident. The consensus of the fusion community seems to be that graphite, although a very attractive material for fusion experiments, cannot be the primary <a href="/wiki/Plasma-facing_material" title="Plasma-facing material">plasma-facing material</a> (PFM) in a commercial reactor.</p>
<p>The sputtering rate of tungsten by the plasma fuel ions is orders of magnitude smaller than that of carbon, and tritium is much less incorporated into redeposited tungsten, making this a more attractive choice. On the other hand, tungsten impurities in a plasma are much more damaging than carbon impurities, and self-sputtering of tungsten can be high, so it will be necessary to ensure that the plasma in contact with the tungsten is not too hot (a few tens of eV rather than hundreds of eV). Tungsten also has disadvantages in terms of eddy currents and melting in off-normal events, as well as some radiological issues.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_the_environment">Safety and the environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Safety and the environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accident_potential">Accident potential</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Accident potential">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nuclear fusion is unlike nuclear fission: fusion requires extremely precise and controlled temperature, pressure and magnetic field parameters for any net energy to be produced. If a reactor suffers damage or loses even a small degree of required control, fusion reactions and heat generation would rapidly cease.<sup id="cite_ref-afraid_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afraid-172">[172]</a></sup> Additionally, fusion reactors contain relatively small amounts of fuel, enough to "burn" for minutes, or in some cases, microseconds. Unless they are actively refueled, the reactions will quickly end. Therefore, fusion reactors are considered extremely safe.<sup id="cite_ref-McCrackenStott2012_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCrackenStott2012-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Runaway reactions cannot occur in a fusion reactor. The <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> is burnt at optimal conditions, and any significant change will quench the reactions. The reaction process is so delicate that this level of safety is inherent. Although the plasma in a fusion power station is expected to have a volume of 1,000 cubic metres (35,000&#160;cu&#160;ft) or more, the plasma density is low and the total amount of fusion fuel in the vessel typically only a few grams.<sup id="cite_ref-McCrackenStott2012_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCrackenStott2012-173">[173]</a></sup> If the fuel supply is closed, the reaction stops within seconds. In comparison, a fission reactor is typically loaded with enough fuel for several months or years, and no additional fuel is necessary to continue the reaction. It is this large amount of fuel that gives rise to the possibility of a meltdown; nothing analogous exists in a fusion reactor.<sup id="cite_ref-n_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the magnetic approach, strong fields are developed in coils that are held in place mechanically by the reactor structure. Failure of this structure could release this tension and allow the magnet to "explode" outward. The severity of this event would be similar to any other industrial accident or an <a href="/wiki/MRI" class="mw-redirect" title="MRI">MRI</a> machine quench/explosion, and could be effectively stopped with a <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a> similar to those used in existing (fission) nuclear generators. The laser-driven inertial approach is generally lower-stress because of the increased size of the reaction chamber. Although failure of the reaction chamber is possible, simply stopping fuel delivery would prevent any sort of catastrophic failure.</p>
<p>Most reactor designs rely on liquid hydrogen as both a coolant and a method for converting stray neutrons from the reaction into <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>, which is fed back into the reactor as fuel. Hydrogen is highly flammable, and in the case of a fire it is possible that the hydrogen stored on-site could be burned up and escape. In this case, the tritium contents of the hydrogen would be released into the atmosphere, posing a radiation risk. Calculations suggest that at about 1&#160;kg the total amount of tritium and other radioactive gases in a typical power station would be so small that they would have diluted to legally acceptable limits by the time they blew as far as the station's <a href="/wiki/Perimeter_fence" title="Perimeter fence">perimeter fence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldEnergyCouncil_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEnergyCouncil-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>The likelihood of <i>small industrial</i> accidents including the local release of radioactivity and injury to staff cannot be estimated yet. These would include accidental releases of lithium or tritium or mis-handling of decommissioned radioactive components of the reactor itself.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnet_quench">Magnet quench</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Magnet quench">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A quench is an abnormal termination of magnet operation that occurs when part of the superconducting coil enters the normal (<a href="/wiki/Resistive" class="mw-redirect" title="Resistive">resistive</a>) state. This can occur because the field inside the magnet is too large, the rate of change of field is too large (causing <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy currents</a> and resultant <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">heating</a> in the copper support matrix), or a combination of the two.</p>
<p>More rarely a defect in the magnet can cause a quench. When this happens, that particular spot is subject to rapid <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">Joule heating</a> from the enormous current, which raises the <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of the surrounding regions. This pushes those regions into the normal state as well, which leads to more heating in a chain reaction. The entire magnet rapidly becomes normal (this can take several seconds, depending on the size of the superconducting coil). This is accompanied by a loud bang as the energy in the magnetic field is converted to heat, and rapid boil-off of the <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> fluid. The abrupt decrease of current can result in kilovolt inductive voltage spikes and arcing. Permanent damage to the magnet is rare, but components can be damaged by localized heating, high voltages, or large mechanical forces.</p>
<p>In practice, magnets usually have safety devices to stop or limit the current when the beginning of a quench is detected.
