If a large magnet undergoes a quench, the inert vapor formed by the evaporating cryogenic fluid can present a significant <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Asphyxiation">asphyxiation</a> hazard to operators by displacing breathable air.</p>
<p>A large section of the superconducting magnets in <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> unexpectedly quenched during start-up operations in 2008, necessitating the replacement of a number of magnets.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In order to mitigate against potentially destructive quenches, the superconducting magnets that form the LHC are equipped with fast-ramping heaters which are activated once a quench event is detected by the complex quench protection system. As the dipole bending magnets are connected in series, each power circuit includes 154 individual magnets, and should a quench event occur, the entire combined stored energy of these magnets must be dumped at once. This energy is transferred into dumps that are massive blocks of metal which heat up to several hundreds of degrees Celsius—because of resistive heating—in a matter of seconds. Although undesirable, a magnet quench is a "fairly routine event" during the operation of a particle accelerator.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effluents">Effluents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Effluents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The natural product of the fusion reaction is a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, which is completely harmless to life. Of more concern is <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>, which, like other isotopes of hydrogen, is difficult to retain completely. During normal operation, some amount of tritium will be continually released.</p>
<p>Although tritium is volatile and biologically active, the health risk posed by a release is much lower than that of most radioactive contaminants, because of tritium's short half-life (12.32 years) and very low decay energy (~14.95 keV), and because it does not <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulate</a> (instead being cycled out of the body as water, with a <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">biological half-life</a> of 7 to 14 days).<sup id="cite_ref-nuclearsafety-petrangeli_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclearsafety-petrangeli-178">[178]</a></sup> Current ITER designs are investigating total containment facilities for any tritium.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_management">Waste management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Waste management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The large flux of high-energy neutrons in a reactor will make the structural materials radioactive. The radioactive inventory at shut-down may be comparable to that of a fission reactor, but there are important differences.</p>
<p>The half-life of the <a href="/wiki/Radioisotopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotopes">radioisotopes</a> produced by fusion tends to be less than those from fission, so that the inventory decreases more rapidly. Unlike fission reactors, whose waste remains radioactive for thousands of years, most of the radioactive material in a fusion reactor would be the reactor core itself, which would be dangerous for about 50 years, and low-level waste for another 100. Although this waste will be considerably more radioactive during those 50 years than fission waste, the very short half-life makes the process very attractive, as the waste management is fairly straightforward. By 500 years the material would have the same radiotoxicity as <a href="/wiki/Coal_ash" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal ash">coal ash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldEnergyCouncil_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEnergyCouncil-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, the choice of materials used in a fusion reactor is less constrained than in a fission design, where many materials are required for their specific <a href="/wiki/Neutron_cross-section" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron cross-section">neutron cross-sections</a>. This allows a fusion reactor to be designed using materials that are selected specifically to be "low activation", materials that do not easily become radioactive. <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">Vanadium</a>, for example, would become much less radioactive than <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon fiber">Carbon fiber</a> materials are also low-activation, as well as being strong and light, and are a promising area of study for laser-inertial reactors where a magnetic field is not required.</p>
<p>In general terms, fusion reactors would create far less radioactive material than a fission reactor, the material it would create is less damaging biologically, and the radioactivity "burns off" within a time period that is well within existing engineering capabilities for safe long-term waste storage.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Nuclear proliferation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a></div>
<p>Although fusion power uses nuclear technology, the overlap with nuclear weapons would be limited. A huge amount of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> could be produced by a fusion power station; tritium is used in the trigger of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bombs</a> and in a modern <a href="/wiki/Boosted_fission_weapon" title="Boosted fission weapon">boosted fission weapon</a>, but it can also be produced by nuclear fission. The energetic neutrons from a fusion reactor could be used to breed weapons-grade <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> for an atomic bomb (for example by transmutation of U<sup>238</sup> to Pu<sup>239</sup>, or Th<sup>232</sup> to U<sup>233</sup>).</p>
<p>A study conducted 2011 assessed the risk of three scenarios:<sup id="cite_ref-ProliferationRisk_Goldston_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProliferationRisk_Goldston-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i>Use in small-scale fusion station</i>: As a result of much higher power consumption, heat dissipation and a more recognizable design compared to enrichment <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">gas centrifuges</a> this choice would be much easier to detect and therefore implausible.<sup id="cite_ref-ProliferationRisk_Goldston_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProliferationRisk_Goldston-179">[179]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Modifications to produce weapon-usable material in a commercial facility:</i> The production potential is significant. But no fertile or fissile substances necessary for the production of weapon-usable materials needs to be present at a civil fusion system at all. If not shielded, a detection of these materials can be done by their characteristic gamma radiation. The underlying redesign could be detected by regular design information verifications. In the (technically more feasible) case of solid breeder blanket modules, it would be necessary for incoming components to be inspected for the presence of fertile material,<sup id="cite_ref-ProliferationRisk_Goldston_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProliferationRisk_Goldston-179">[179]</a></sup> otherwise plutonium for several weapons could be produced each year.<sup id="cite_ref-StrongNeutronSources_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StrongNeutronSources-180">[180]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Prioritizing a fast production of weapon-grade material regardless of secrecy:</i> The fastest way to produce weapon usable material was seen in modifying a prior civil fusion power station. Unlike in some nuclear power stations, there is no weapon compatible material during civil use. Even without the need for covert action this modification would still take about 2 months to start the production and at least an additional week to generate a significant amount for weapon production. This was seen as enough time to detect a military use and to react with diplomatic or military means. To stop the production, a military destruction of inevitable parts of the facility leaving out the reactor itself would be sufficient. This, together with the intrinsic safety of fusion power would only bear a low risk of radioactive contamination.<sup id="cite_ref-ProliferationRisk_Goldston_179-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProliferationRisk_Goldston-179">[179]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Another study concludes that "[..]large fusion reactors – even if not designed for fissile material breeding – could easily produce several hundred kg Pu per year with high weapon quality and very low source material requirements." It was emphasized that the implementation of features for intrinsic proliferation resistance might only be possible at this phase of research and development.<sup id="cite_ref-StrongNeutronSources_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StrongNeutronSources-180">[180]</a></sup> The theoretical and computational tools needed for hydrogen bomb design are closely related to those needed for <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement fusion</a>, but have very little in common with the more scientifically developed <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_confinement_fusion" title="Magnetic confinement fusion">magnetic confinement fusion</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_source">Energy source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Energy source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large-scale reactors using neutronic fuels (e.g. <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a>) and thermal power production (turbine based) are most comparable to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">fission power</a> from an engineering and economics viewpoint. Both fission and fusion power stations involve a relatively compact heat source powering a conventional steam turbine-based power station, while producing enough neutron radiation to make <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">activation</a> of the station materials problematic. The main distinction is that fusion power produces no high-level radioactive waste (though activated station materials still need to be disposed of). There are some power station ideas that may significantly lower the cost or size of such stations; however, research in these areas is nowhere near as advanced as in <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamaks</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Fusion power commonly proposes the use of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of hydrogen, as fuel and in many current designs also use <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>. Assuming a fusion energy output equal to the 1995 global power output of about 100 <a href="/wiki/Exa-" title="Exa-">E</a>J/yr (= 1 × 10<sup>20</sup> J/yr) and that this does not increase in the future, which is unlikely, then the known current lithium reserves would last 3000 years. Lithium from sea water would last 60 million years, however, and a more complicated fusion process using only deuterium from sea water would have fuel for 150 billion years.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> To put this in context, 150 billion years is close to 30 times the remaining lifespan of the sun,<sup id="cite_ref-sunlife_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sunlife-182">[182]</a></sup> and more than 10 times the estimated age of the universe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While fusion power is still in early stages of development, substantial sums have been and continue to be invested in research. In the EU almost <span class="nowrap">€10 billion</span> was spent on fusion research up to the end of the 1990s, and the new <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> reactor alone is budgeted at <span class="nowrap">€6.6 billion total for the timeframe between 2008 and 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></span></p>
<p>It is estimated that up to the point of possible implementation of electricity generation by nuclear fusion, R&amp;D will need further promotion totalling around <span class="nowrap">€60–80 billion</span> over a period of <span class="nowrap">50 years</span> or so (of which <span class="nowrap">€20–30 billion</span> within the EU) based on a report from 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Nuclear fusion research receives <span class="nowrap">€750 million</span> (excluding ITER funding) from the European Union, compared with <span class="nowrap">€810 million</span> for sustainable energy research,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> putting research into fusion power well ahead of that of any single rivaling technology. Indeed, the size of the investments and time frame of the expected results mean that fusion research is almost exclusively publicly funded, while research in other forms of energy can be done by the private sector. In spite of that, a number of start-up companies active in the field of fusion power have managed to attract private money.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages">Advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Fusion power would provide more energy for a given weight of fuel than any fuel-consuming energy source currently in use,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> and the fuel itself (primarily <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>) exists abundantly in the Earth's ocean: about 1 in 6500 hydrogen atoms in seawater is deuterium.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Although this may seem a low proportion (about 0.015%), because nuclear fusion reactions are so much more energetic than chemical combustion and seawater is easier to access and more plentiful than fossil fuels, fusion could potentially supply the world's energy needs for millions of years.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite being technically <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-renewable energy">non-renewable</a>, fusion power has many of the benefits of renewable energy sources (such as being a long-term energy supply and emitting no <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>) as well as some of the benefits of the resource-limited energy sources as hydrocarbons and nuclear fission (without <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a>). Like these currently dominant energy sources, fusion could provide very high power-generation density and uninterrupted power delivery (because it is not dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>, unlike wind and solar power).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Another aspect of fusion energy is that the cost of production does not suffer from <a href="/wiki/Diseconomies_of_scale" title="Diseconomies of scale">diseconomies of scale</a>. The cost of water and wind energy, for example, goes up as the optimal locations are developed first, while further generators must be sited in less ideal conditions. With fusion energy the production cost will not increase much even if large numbers of stations are built, because the raw resource (seawater) is abundant and widespread.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Some problems that are expected to be an issue in this century, such as <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">fresh water shortages</a>, can alternatively be regarded as problems of energy supply. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a> stations, <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> can be purified through <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reverse_osmosis" title="Reverse osmosis">reverse osmosis</a>. Nonetheless, these processes are energy intensive. Even if the first fusion stations are not competitive with alternative sources, fusion could still become competitive if large-scale desalination requires more power than the alternatives are able to provide.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A scenario has been presented of the effect of the commercialization of fusion power on the future of human civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> ITER and later <a href="/wiki/DEMO" title="DEMO">DEMO</a> are envisioned to bring online the first commercial nuclear fusion energy reactor by 2050. Using this as the starting point and the history of the uptake of nuclear fission reactors as a guide, the scenario depicts a rapid take up of nuclear fusion energy starting after the middle of this century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Fusion power could be used in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a>, where solar energy is not available.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_and_lack_of_feasibility">Predictions and lack of feasibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fusion_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Predictions and lack of feasibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since the 1950s, predictions of technical feasible use of hot fusion for electricity production have been around 30–50 years from the respective presents. A German insider joke is to call that effect Fusionskonstante ("fusion constant", similar as in <a href="/wiki/Oil_constant" title="Oil constant">oil constant</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> From the very start, fusion power projects involved various cost overruns and missed deadlines. For example, the construction of the National Ignition Facility cost $5 billion and took seven years longer than expected. ITER's expected cost has gone from $5 billion to $20 billion, and the date for full power operation has been put back to 2027, from the original estimate of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
